Analogy of a Prophet Foretelling Future Events
Nasafi uses an analogy of a prophet foretelling an event to prove the same principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses an analogy of a prophet's foretelling to argue that an action's intention depends on its outcome, not the act itself.

Core Idea
Intention behind actions is judged by their purpose, not the act alone.

Nasafi continues his refutation by presenting a powerful analogy. He asks his opponents: if a prophet foretells a harmful event, does he desire it to happen or not? The answer forces them to admit that an action's ethical value depends on its consequences. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy
Read the hypothetical scenario carefully. Understand the two options given to the opponents.
The prophet is the subject, the disbeliever is the agent.
2
Examine the first option
If they say the prophet desires the event, they concede that desiring something apparently foolish (harm to oneself) is not foolish if it serves a higher purpose (proving prophethood).
This is the key point.
3
Examine the second option
If they say the prophet desires it not to happen, then they accuse the prophet of lying, which contradicts their own belief in his truthfulness.
This exposes their obstinacy.
4
Connect to the broader argument
The author notes that the objection about intending one's own foolishness has already been answered in the discussion on creation of actions.
This ties the argument to previous topics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم: لو أن رسولاً يخبر قومه أن فلانا الكافر يجيئني فيشتمني ويقصد قتلي أيريد تحقيق ذلك ليظهر صدقة في إخباره عن الغيب فيكون معجزة له أم يريد أن لا يوجد ذلك فيظهر كذبه ولا تتحقق معجزته؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فقد أقروا أن مريد السفه لا يكون سفيهًا إذا كانت لذلك عاقبة حميدة، وإن قالوا بالثاني ظهر للناس تعنتهم ومكابرتهم، وما قالوا إن مريد سفه نفسه في الشاهد سفه، فقد سبق جوابه في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
And it is said to them: Suppose a messenger informs his people that such-and-such a disbeliever will come to me, insult me, and intend to kill me—does he intend the realization of that so that his truthfulness in informing about the unseen appears, making it a miracle for him, or does he intend that it not happen so that his lie appears and his miracle is not realized? If they say: The first, then they have affirmed that the one who intends foolishness is not foolish if it has a praiseworthy outcome. And if they say: The second, then their obstinacy and contrariness appear to the people. And as to their claim that the one who intends foolishness itself in the witnessed realm is foolish, its answer has already preceded in the issue of the creation of actions.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage continues the same argument with a new analogy. The author presents a hypothetical scenario: a prophet tells his people that a specific disbeliever will come to him, insult him, and try to kill him. Does the prophet desire that event to occur (so that his prophecy is fulfilled and a miracle is established) or does he desire it not to occur (so that he is shown to be a liar)? The opponents are given two options. If they say the prophet desires the event, they concede that desiring something foolish (being insulted) is not foolish when it has a good outcome (proving prophethood). If they say the prophet desires the event not to occur, then they attribute obstinacy to the people and refute their own position. The author then notes that the objection about intending one's own foolishness in the witnessed world has already been answered in the discussion on the creation of actions (khalq al-af'al).

Key Terms
رسول
غيب
معجزة
صدقة (صدق)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a prophet cannot desire his own harm, but the lesson shows it can be wise if it serves a higher purpose.
Correction: The analogy demonstrates that desiring apparent harm is not intrinsically foolish; the intention is judged by the outcome.

Checkpoint
In the analogy, what would the opponents have to concede if they say the prophet desires the event to occur?
A) That the prophet is foolish.
B) That the prophet is a liar.
C) That desiring foolishness with a good outcome is not foolish.
D) That the people are obstinate.

Key Takeaway
An action's moral evaluation depends on its intended outcome, not just its apparent harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time you desired something that outwardly seemed harmful to you, but you pursued it for a higher purpose. Did you trust that Allah would bring good from it? How does this lesson call you to re-evaluate the intentions behind your own difficult choices?
?
In your daily life, do you judge your own actions—or others'—solely by their apparent harm or benefit? What would it mean for you to adopt the deeper perspective this lesson teaches, looking at the ultimate purpose? Commit to one specific area where you will apply this principle starting today.
?
Consider your attitude toward trials and suffering. Have you ever seen a trial as pointless? This lesson shows that even the prophet's own desire for a harmful event was wise because of its outcome. How can you learn to trust Allah's wisdom in the trials you face, even when you do not see the immediate good?

Prophethood

Absurd Consequence of Mu'tazila Doctrine on Prophets
The author exposes the absurd implication of the Mu'tazila al-aslah doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila principle of al-aslah leads to an absurd conclusion regarding the prophets, and how the author refutes it by reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's 'most beneficial' principle absurdly implies that the death of prophets is better than their life.

This passage presents a devastating critique of the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aslah. The author forces the doctrine to its logical extreme, showing that it would mean God should have ended the lives of all prophets and righteous people at the earliest opportunity, while preserving Iblis and the wicked. The sheer absurdity of this conclusion demonstrates the unsustainability of the Mu'tazila position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the strong emotional language at the end.
2
Understand the reductio argument
Identify the logical consequence the author draws from Mu'tazila principles. What is the assumption? What conclusion follows?
The argument is: if God must do what is most beneficial, then the death of Muhammad at that hour was most beneficial, because living longer would cause disbelief.
3
Consider the implications for all righteous
The author extends the argument to every messenger, saint, and righteous person. Ask yourself: why does this make the doctrine absurd?
4
Reflect on the author's condemnation
The author strongly condemns this view as unbearable for a believer. Consider why such strong language is used in theological argumentation.
This shows the gravity of the doctrinal error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم مآل مذهبهم إلى أن الله تعالى لو بقى محمدًا عليه السلام لمحة بصر وراء الساعة التي قبض روحه فيها لكفر الله تعالى وعصاه وكان قبض روحه في تلك الساعة أصلح له، وكذا في كل رسول ونبي وولي وصديق، فكان بقاء الرسل والأخيار مفسدة لهم وللخلق وبقاء إبليس والأشرار أصلح لهم وللخلق، وهذا قول لا يحتمل قلب مؤمن سماعه لفظاعته في نفسه وبشاعته في ذاته، والله خصيم من هذا قوله في رسله وأنبيائه المصطفين الأخيار وأوليائه وعباده الأبرار، ونسأل الله العصمة من الضلال والتمادي في الغي والجهالة.
Then the consequence of their doctrine is that if God Most High had kept Muhammad, peace be upon him, for a blink of an eye beyond the hour in which He took his soul, then God would have been disbelieved and disobeyed, and taking his soul at that hour was most beneficial for him. And likewise for every messenger, prophet, saint, and truthful one. So the continued existence of messengers and the righteous is corruption for them and for creation, and the continued existence of Iblis and the wicked is most beneficial for them and for creation. This is a statement that no believing heart can bear to hear due to its atrocity in itself and its ugliness in essence. And God is the adversary of anyone who says this about His messengers, His chosen prophets, the righteous, His saints, and His pious servants. And we ask God for protection from misguidance and persistence in error and ignorance.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage shows the reductio ad absurdum of the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial). The author argues that if the principle is that God must do what is most beneficial for each individual, then for the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, it would have been most beneficial for God to take his soul at the exact moment he did, because if he lived even a moment longer, people would have disbelieved and disobeyed. This same reasoning applies to all prophets, saints, and righteous people—their continued existence is considered corruption (mafsada) for them and for creation, while the continued existence of Iblis and the wicked is considered most beneficial. The author declares this statement so atrocious and ugly that no believer can bear to hear it, and invokes God as the adversary of anyone who says such things about His chosen servants. He ends with a prayer for protection from misguidance.

Key Terms
الأصلح
المفسدة
الرسل
الأخيار
الأشرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila only apply the principle of al-aslah to worldly matters and not to matters of life and death.
Correction: The principle, as argued by the author, logically extends to all aspects, including the timing of death, leading to absurd conclusions.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the Mu'tazila doctrine imply about the Prophet Muhammad's death?
A) It was a punishment for him.
B) It was most beneficial for him because living longer would cause disbelief.
C) It was arbitrary and not based on benefit.
D) It was a test of his faith.

Key Takeaway
The reductio ad absurdum is a powerful tool to expose the logical flaws in a theological doctrine, and the Mu'tazila principle of al-aslah fails when applied consistently to the lives of prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone say that God must do what is most beneficial for us, do you ever stop to consider where that logic leads? Have you ever relied on your own sense of what is best instead of submitting to what God has decreed, even when it seems harsh? What would it take for you to trust God's wisdom over your own calculations?
?
The author says no believing heart can bear this statement. Is your heart still sensitive enough to shudder at the thought that the Prophet's life could be considered less beneficial than his death? Or have you become desensitized to subtle disrespect toward the prophets? Ask yourself today: Do you truly honour the Prophet more than your own reasoning?
?
If the Mu'tazila could go so wrong by applying a seemingly good principle to the prophets, how much more careful must you be when evaluating your own 'logical' conclusions about religion? Have you ever justified a belief by saying 'it only makes sense' without checking scripture? What can you do to guard your heart from such misguidance?
